    <meta name="journal_id" content='13045'/>
    
    <meta name="dc.title" content='Targeting histone methylation for cancer therapy: enzymes, inhibitors, biological activity and perspectives'/>
    
    <meta name="dc.description" content='Post-translational methylation of histone lysine or arginine residues plays important roles in gene regulation and other physiological processes. Aberrant histone methylation caused by a gene mutation, translocation, or overexpression can often lead to initiation of a disease such as cancer. Small molecule inhibitors of such histone modifying enzymes that correct the abnormal methylation could be used as novel therapeutics for these diseases, or as chemical probes for investigation of epigenetics. Discovery and development of histone methylation modulators are in an early stage and undergo a rapid expansion in the past few years. A number of highly potent and selective compounds have been reported, together with extensive preclinical studies of their biological activity. Several compounds have been in clinical trials for safety, pharmacokinetics, and efficacy, targeting several types of cancer. This review summarizes the biochemistry, structures, and biology of cancer-relevant histone methylation modifying enzymes, small molecule inhibitors and their preclinical and clinical antitumor activities. Perspectives for targeting histone methylation for cancer therapy are also discussed.'/>

    <meta name="prism.doi" content='10.1186/s13045-016-0279-9'/>

    <meta name="citation_doi" content='10.1186/s13045-016-0279-9'/>
    
    <meta name="citation_abstract" content='&lt;h2&gt;Abstract&lt;/h2&gt;Post-translational methylation of histone lysine or arginine residues plays important roles in gene regulation and other physiological processes. Aberrant histone methylation caused by a gene mutation, translocation, or overexpression can often lead to initiation of a disease such as cancer. Small molecule inhibitors of such histone modifying enzymes that correct the abnormal methylation could be used as novel therapeutics for these diseases, or as chemical probes for investigation of epigenetics. Discovery and development of histone methylation modulators are in an early stage and undergo a rapid expansion in the past few years. A number of highly potent and selective compounds have been reported, together with extensive preclinical studies of their biological activity. Several compounds have been in clinical trials for safety, pharmacokinetics, and efficacy, targeting several types of cancer. This review summarizes the biochemistry, structures, and biology of cancer-relevant histone methylation modifying enzymes, small molecule inhibitors and their preclinical and clinical antitumor activities. Perspectives for targeting histone methylation for cancer therapy are also discussed.'/>

                            <div class="FulltextWrapper"><div xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" class="MainTitleSection"><h1 xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="ArticleTitle" lang="en">Targeting histone methylation for cancer therapy: enzymes, inhibitors, biological activity and perspectives</h1></div><div xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" class="AuthorNames" data-author-affiliations="true"><ul class="u-listReset"><li class="Author" data-jumpto="Aff"><span xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="AuthorName">Yongcheng&nbsp;Song</span><sup><a href="#Aff1" class="AffiliationID">1</a>, <a href="#Aff2" class="AffiliationID">2</a></sup><a href="mailto:ysong@bcm.edu" class="EmailAuthor" title="Email author"><span class="ContactIcon" aria-hidden="true"></span><span class="u-srOnly">Email author</span></a>, </li><li class="Author" data-jumpto="Aff"><span xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="AuthorName">Fangrui&nbsp;Wu</span><sup><a href="#Aff1" class="AffiliationID">1</a></sup> and </li><li class="Author" data-jumpto="Aff"><span xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="AuthorName">Jingyu&nbsp;Wu</span><sup><a href="#Aff1" class="AffiliationID">1</a></sup></li></ul></div><div xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" class="ArticleContextInformation"><div class="ContextInformation"><div class="ContextInformationJournalTitles"><span class="ArticleCitation"><span xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="JournalTitle">Journal of Hematology &amp; Oncology</span><span class="ArticleCitation_Year">2016</span><span class="ArticleCitation_Volume"><strong>9</strong>:49</span></span></div><p class="ArticleDOI"><a target="_blank" href="https://doi.org/10.1186/s13045-016-0279-9">https://doi.org/10.1186/s13045-016-0279-9</a></p><p class="Copyright">© &nbsp;Song et al.&nbsp;2016</p></div></div><div class="ArticleHistory"><p class="History HistoryReceived"><strong>Received: </strong>6&nbsp;April&nbsp;2016</p><p class="History HistoryAccepted"><strong>Accepted: </strong>7&nbsp;June&nbsp;2016</p><p class="History HistoryOnlineDate"><strong>Published: </strong>17&nbsp;June&nbsp;2016</p></div></div>
                            
                            
                            
    

                            <div class="FulltextWrapper"><section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" class="Abstract" id="Abs1" lang="en"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Abstract</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par1" class="Para">Post-translational methylation of histone lysine or arginine residues plays important roles in gene regulation and other physiological processes. Aberrant histone methylation caused by a gene mutation, translocation, or overexpression can often lead to initiation of a disease such as cancer. Small molecule inhibitors of such histone modifying enzymes that correct the abnormal methylation could be used as novel therapeutics for these diseases, or as chemical probes for investigation of epigenetics. Discovery and development of histone methylation modulators are in an early stage and undergo a rapid expansion in the past few years. A number of highly potent and selective compounds have been reported, together with extensive preclinical studies of their biological activity. Several compounds have been in clinical trials for safety, pharmacokinetics, and efficacy, targeting several types of cancer. This review summarizes the biochemistry, structures, and biology of cancer-relevant histone methylation modifying enzymes, small molecule inhibitors and their preclinical and clinical antitumor activities. Perspectives for targeting histone methylation for cancer therapy are also discussed.</p></div></section><div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" class="KeywordGroup" lang="en">
              <h3 xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" class="Heading">Keywords</h3>
              <span class="Keyword">Histone methylation</span>
              <span class="Keyword">Enzyme inhibitor</span>
              <span class="Keyword">Histone lysine methyltransferase</span>
              <span class="Keyword">Protein arginine methyltransferase</span>
              <span class="Keyword">Histone demethylase</span>
              <span class="Keyword">Cancer therapeutics</span>
            </div>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec1" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Background</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par2" class="Para">Nucleosome is the smallest structural unit of the human genetic material, which is composed of ~146 base pairs of double-stranded DNA wrapped around a histone octamer that contains two copies of histone H2A, H2B, H3, and H4 proteins. Basic lysine and arginine residues are enriched in histones. At physiological pH, these positively charged sidechains provide strong electrostatic and H-bond interactions with the negatively charged DNA for tight binding and packaging. Chromatin, a linear array of millions of nucleosomes, is organized into higher orders of structure and tightly condensed to form a chromosome. Functionally, chromatin is classified into highly packed, transcriptionally inactive heterochromatin and transcriptionally active euchromatin, whose structure is less condensed and DNA is therefore more accessible to a transcription machinery [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR1" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR1">1</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR2" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR2">2</a></span>]. Post-translational modifications of histones, such as acetylation and methylation, largely control DNA accessibility and regulate gene expression. For example, acetylation can neutralize the positive charged lysine sidechain and render a more open DNA structure to facilitate the binding of transcription factors as well as other proteins for gene expression. Abnormal histone modifications often occur in many diseases such as cancer. Histone modifying enzymes are therefore potential drug targets for these diseases [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR3" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR3">3</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR4" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR4">4</a></span>]. Small molecule inhibitors of histone deacetylases (HDAC) have been extensively developed and several compounds, such as vorinostat and romidepsin, have been approved to treat T-cell lymphomas [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR5" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR5">5</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR7" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR7">7</a></span>]. Moreover, HDAC inhibitors have been in many clinical trials for other hematologic and solid cancers, with &gt;500 studies in clinicaltrials.gov.</p><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par3" class="Para">Physiological and pathological functions of histone methylation in a lysine or arginine residue have been well studied and documented. These post-translational modifications play crucial roles in gene regulation, cell differentiation, DNA recombination, and damage repair in normal cells as well as pathogenesis in diseases [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR4" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR4">4</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR8" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR8">8</a></span>]. A large family of ≥60 histone methyltransferases (HMT), including histone lysine methyltransferases (HKMT) and protein/histone arginine methyltransferases (PRMT), were identified in humans, among which the biochemical and biological functions of many methyltransferases have been characterized [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR9" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR9">9</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR10" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR10">10</a></span>]. In addition, histone methylation is dynamically controlled by histone/protein lysine demethylases (KDM), enzymes that remove the methyl group(s) from a methylated lysine sidechain [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR11" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR11">11</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR13" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR13">13</a></span>]. The opposite functions between HMTs and KDMs facilitate to maintain balanced histone methylation levels. Aberrant histone methylations have been frequently found in cancer [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR4" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR4">4</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR8" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR8">8</a></span>], caused by a gene mutation, translocation, or dysregulated expression. Therefore, many HMTs and KDMs are potential drug targets and small molecule inhibitors of these proteins are useful chemical probes or potential therapeutics. As compared to that of HDAC inhibitors, development of histone methylation modulators has been in an early stage [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR4" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR4">4</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR14" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR14">14</a></span>]. There were very few potent inhibitors of HMTs and KDMs before 2010. Significantly more efforts from the academia and pharmaceutical industry have been observed during the past few years, leading to a rapidly increased number of small molecule modulators of histone methylation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR17" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR17">17</a></span>]. Several potent and selective compounds have recently been in clinical trials against acute myeloid leukemia (AML), non-Hodgkin lymphoma and lung cancer, showing the great potential for this class of compounds in cancer therapy.</p><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par4" class="Para">This review summarizes the biochemistry, structures, and biology of histone methylation modifying enzymes, small molecule inhibitors and their preclinical and clinical antitumor activities. Due to the large number of these proteins, only those highly relevant to cancer are described, with a particular focus on histone H3 lysine 79 (H3K79) methyltransferase DOT1L, H3K4 targeting mixed lineage leukemia (MLL) and lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1), and H3K27 methyltransferase EZH2. We also include mutations of isocitrate dehydrogenases (IDH), which have recently been found in 20–80&nbsp;% of gliomas, AML and several types of sarcomas. The mutant IDH proteins indirectly inhibit a broad range of histone demethylases and cause genome-wide histone hypermethylation. In addition, perspectives of targeting histone methylation for cancer therapy are discussed.</p></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec2" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Biochemistry and structure</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par5" class="Para">HMTs belong to a superfamily of methyltransferases containing &gt;100 members from bacteria to humans [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR9" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR9">9</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR10" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR10">10</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR18" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR18">18</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR19" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR19">19</a></span>]. In addition to the lysine and arginine sidechains of a protein, methyltransferases can methylate DNA, RNA and even small molecules such as a catecholamine. The methyl acceptors for these enzymes can be a N (e.g., –NH<sub>2</sub> of a lysine), C (e.g., C5-cytosine in DNA), or O (e.g., –OH of a catecholamine) atom. All methyltransferases use S-adenosylmethionine (SAM) as the enzyme cofactor, with its methyl group (activated by the sulfonium) being the donor. Figure&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig1">1a</a></span> schematically illustrates the general mechanism of catalysis of an HMT. SAM and the substrate histone lysine bind to different binding pockets of HMT in an orientation that brings the methyl donor and acceptor atoms to a close proximity, which facilitates the ensuing nucleophilic substitution reaction to occur, producing the methylated histone and S-adenosylhomocysteine (SAH).<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig1"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO1">
                    <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig1_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig1_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                  </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 1</span>
                      <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Mechanism and structures of histone methyltransferases (HMT). <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">a</strong> Mechanism of catalysis for HMTs. Upon binding to a HMT, the histone lysine NH<sub xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">2</sub> group undergoes a nucleophilic attack to the methyl group of SAM, producing a methylated lysine and SAH; <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">b</strong> The overall structure of DOT1L-SAM complex (PDB: 1NW3) and the close-up view of its active site; <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">c</strong> The overall structure of G9a-SAH complex (PDB: 3K5K) and the close-up view of its active site. SAM/SAH are shown as tube models with their C atoms in green</p>
                    </div></figcaption></figure>
                     </div><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par6" class="Para">Based on X-ray crystallographic studies, there are five classes of methyltransferases with distinct structural features [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR9" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR9">9</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR10" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR10">10</a></span>]. It is of interest that H3K79 methyltransferase DOT1L and all PRMTs belong to class I methyltransferases and share high similarities. However, all other HKMTs are class V methyltransferases containing a SET (<strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">S</strong>u(var)3-9, <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">E</strong>nhancer-of-zeste, <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">T</strong>rithorax) domain, having a distinct structure from DOT1L. Figure&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig1">1b, c</a></span> show the overall structures as well as the close-up views of the active sites of DOT1L and H3K9 methyltransferase G9a, respectively. DOT1L is a typical class I methyltransferase [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR20" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR20">20</a></span>], characterized by an overall protein structure of 7-stranded β sheets flanked by several α helices, as well as an extended binding conformation of SAM (Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig1">1b</a></span>). The SAM-binding pocket is deeply buried inside the protein. While there has been no structural information as to how nucleosome (the substrate) binds to DOT1L, the substrate-binding pocket is largely separated from that of SAM, interconnected by a narrow lysine binding channel. The class V methyltransferase G9a is a structurally distinct protein, consisting of mostly β sheets interlinked by loops (Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig1">1c</a></span>) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR21" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR21">21</a></span>]. SAM/SAH adopts a kinked binding conformation in this class of HMTs.</p><div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par7" class="Para">For the opposite reaction, there are two families of KDMs that can oxidatively remove the methyl group from a methylated lysine sidechain using distinct mechanisms. LSD1 (also known as KDM1A) and its homolog LSD2 (also known as KDM1B) are flavin adenine dinucleotide (FAD) dependent monoamine oxidases (MAO) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR22" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR22">22</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR23" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR23">23</a></span>]. As shown in Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig2">2a</a></span>, the methyl group of a methylated lysine is oxidized by the cofactor FAD to form an imine intermediate, which hydrolyzes to give the demethylated product and formaldehyde. FADH<sub>2</sub>, the reduced form of the cofactor, is oxidized by O<sub>2</sub> to generate FAD and H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> to complete a catalytic cycle. Because the formation of an imine intermediate is required, LSD1/2 can only demethylate a mono- or di-methylated lysine, but not a tri-methylated lysine. Figure&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig2">2b</a></span> shows the X-ray crystal structure of LSD1 in complex with FAD and its H3K4 peptide substrate (with K4M mutation) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR24" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR24">24</a></span>]. FAD is tightly bound inside LSD1, with its aromatic tricyclic flavin ring being part of the large substrate-binding pocket, which can accommodate and recognize the histone H3 peptide. The sidechain of Lys4 (mutated to Met) residue is located in proximity to the flavin ring for oxidation.<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig2"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO2">
                    <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig2_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig2_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                  </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 2</span>
                      <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Mechanisms and structures of histone lysine demethylases (KDM). <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">a</strong> Mechanism of catalysis for FAD dependent KDM1 proteins (including LSD1 and 2); <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">b</strong> The active site of LSD1 in complex with FAD and a histone H3 peptide (PDB: 2V1D). LSD1 is shown as a 50&nbsp;% transparent electrostatic surface. The peptide is shown as a wire model (C atoms in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic">green</em>), with the K4M residue highlighted as a tube model; <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">c</strong> Mechanism of catalysis for JmjC domain KDMs; <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">d</strong> The active site of PHF8 in complex with Fe<sup xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">2+</sup> (<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic">cyan sphere</em>), an α-KG analog (<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic">brown</em>) and a histone H3 peptide (<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic">green</em>) (PDB: 3KV4)</p>
                    </div></figcaption></figure>
                     </div><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par8" class="Para">The other family, consisting of ~30 KDMs including KDM2 - 7 and PHF (plant homeodomain finger) in humans, all contain a JmjC domain and are Fe(II) and α-ketoglutarate (α-KG) dependent dioxygenases [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR13" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR13">13</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR25" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR25">25</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR26" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR26">26</a></span>]. Figure&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig2">2c</a></span> illustrates the general mechanism of catalysis for these enzymes [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR27" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR27">27</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR28" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR28">28</a></span>]. An oxygen molecule coordinates to Fe(II) and oxidizes both Fe(II) and α-KG to give (upon decarboxylation) a succinate and Fe(IV)-oxo intermediate. Next, the Fe(IV) species oxidizes the C atom of the methylated lysine to form a hydroxymethylamine intermediate, which hydrolyzes to produce the demethylated product and formaldehyde. Unlike LSD1/2, the JmjC family of KDMs can demethylate mono-, di-, and tri-methylated lysine. Figure&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig2">2d</a></span> shows the X-ray structure of PHF8 in complex with Fe<sup>2+</sup>, an α-KG analog and a methylated histone H3 peptide [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR29" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR29">29</a></span>]. The central metal ion is coordinated with an Asp and two His residues, together with two O atoms of α-KG. One of the methyl groups of H3K9me2 sidechain is located closely to the metal ion.</p><div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par9" class="Para">To date, six lysine residues in histone H3 and H4, i.e., H3K4, K9, K27, K36, K79, and H4K20, have been found to be methylated. Figure&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig3">3</a></span> illustrates the substrate-specificity of HKMs and KDMs. Many other lysine residues, including H3K14, 18, 23, and H4K5, 8, 12, and 16, are not methylated. Rather, they can be acetylated. It is also of interest that H3K9 and K27 can also be acetylated. Mutually exclusive acetylation or methylation at H3K9 and K27 appears to play drastically distinct physiological functions. Acetylated H3K9 and K27 cause activated gene transcription, while methylated H3K9 and K27 are transcriptional repressive.<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig3"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO3">
                    <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig3_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig3_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                  </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 3</span>
                      <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Histone H3 and H4 lysine substrate-specificity of HMTs and KDMs</p>
                    </div></figcaption></figure>
                     </div></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec3" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">H3K79 methyltransferase DOT1L</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><section id="Sec4" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">DOT1L’s physiological function and pathogenesis in leukemia</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par10" class="Para">DOT1L (<strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">d</strong>isruptor <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">o</strong>f <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">t</strong>elomeric silencing 1 <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">l</strong>ike) was identified as a human homolog of yeast DOT1, which was found to disrupt telomeric silencing in budding yeast in a genetic screen [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR30" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR30">30</a></span>]. The full-length human DOT1L has 1537 amino acids, with its highly conserved N-terminal domain of ~360 amino acids being an H3K79 methyltransferase [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR31" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR31">31</a></span>]. The remaining part of mammalian DOT1L is involved in interactions with many transcription proteins, such as AF4, AF9, AF10, and ENL [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR32" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR32">32</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR36" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR36">36</a></span>]. Biology of DOT1L in health and diseases has been summarized in several recent reviews [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR37" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR37">37</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR39" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR39">39</a></span>]. The biological function of DOT1L (as well as DOT1) is to methylate H3K79 as part of a transcription complex, which can initiate or maintain an active transcription state. This is supported by the genetic studies in yeast, showing ~10&nbsp;% genome containing hypomethylated H3K79 are located at transcriptionally inactive loci, while the remaining 90&nbsp;% genes with an H3K79 methyl marker are actively transcribed [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR40" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR40">40</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR41" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR41">41</a></span>]. This also occurs in <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">Drosophila</em> and mammals [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR42" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR42">42</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR43" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR43">43</a></span>]. Several large transcription protein complexes containing DOT1L have been purified and identified using chromatin immunoprecipitation, including ENL-associated proteins (EAP), DOT1L-containing complex (DotCom), and Super elongation complex (SEC) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR32" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR32">32</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR36" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR36">36</a></span>]. Several transcription relevant proteins were repeatedly present in these complexes, including transcription factors AF4, AF9, AF10, and ENL, as well as P-TEFb kinase. P-TEFb is a cyclin-dependent kinase that can phosphorylates RNA polymerase II, which is required for transcription elongation. These strongly support DOT1L as well as H3K79 methylation is crucial to gene transcription.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par11" class="Para">DOT1L plays important roles in normal physiology of an organism. For embryonic development, methylation at H3K79 is absent in the very early stage and increasing levels of H3K79me2 can be found in later stages, suggesting this “histone code” is important for embryonic development [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR44" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR44">44</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR45" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR45">45</a></span>]. Germline knockout of mouse DOT1L was embryonic lethal and major defects in the cardiovascular system were found in the knockout embryos [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR46" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR46">46</a></span>]. Additionally, DOT1L has been found to be crucial for maintaining normal hematopoiesis in mice [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR47" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR47">47</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR48" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR48">48</a></span>]. Conditional knockout of DOT1L in bone marrow significantly decreased hematopoietic stem cells as well as all types of progenitor cells. Moreover, other studies have shown DOT1L plays roles in maintaining normal functions of heart and kidney [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR46" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR46">46</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR49" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR49">49</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR51" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR51">51</a></span>].</p>
                <div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par12" class="Para">DOT1L has been found to be a drug target for acute leukemia with a mixed lineage leukemia (MLL, also known as MLL1 or KMT2A) gene translocation. This subtype of leukemia accounts for ~75&nbsp;% of acute leukemia in infants and ~10&nbsp;% in children and adults [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR52" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR52">52</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR54" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR54">54</a></span>] with a particularly poor prognosis [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR55" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR55">55</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR58" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR58">58</a></span>]. The phenotype of MLL-rearranged leukemia can be acute myeloid leukemia (AML), acute lymphoid leukemia (ALL), or mixed lineage leukemia. However, despite phenotypic differences, gene profiling showed these MLL-rearranged leukemias share a similar gene expression signature [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR59" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR59">59</a></span>]. The biology of MLL and leukemogenesis of MLL-rearranged oncogenes have been well studied and reviewed [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR60" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR60">60</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR62" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR62">62</a></span>]. Briefly, MLL is a large, multi-domain protein (3969 amino acids), containing an N-terminal AT hook domain that recognizes and binds to DNA as well as a C-terminal SET domain that is an H3K4 methyltransferase [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR52" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR52">52</a></span>]. Figure&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig4">4a</a></span> schematically illustrates the biology of MLL for gene expression in normal cells. Upon binding to the promoter region of its target genes, the SET domain of MLL can methylate H3K4, which also represents a histone marker for active gene transcription [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR63" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR63">63</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR64" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR64">64</a></span>]. In the leukemia, the chromosome rearrangement replaces the C-terminal part of MLL with a fusion partner gene [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR52" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR52">52</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR53" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR53">53</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR65" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR65">65</a></span>]. The SET domain as well as its H3K4 methylation activity is thus lost. To date, although &gt;70 partner genes have been documented, onco-MLLs fused with transcription factors AF4, AF9, AF10, and ENL account for the majority (&gt;70&nbsp;%). As shown in Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig4">4b</a></span>, these four proteins are able to recruit DOT1L into the MLL transcription complex, which subsequently methylates H3K79 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR32" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR32">32</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR34" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR34">34</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR51" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR51">51</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR66" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR66">66</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR67" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR67">67</a></span>]. This aberrant epigenetic event dysregulates the expression of many MLL target genes, such as HoxA9, HoxA7, and Meis1 whose overexpression can cause leukemia. Abnormal H3K79 methylation has been observed in the clinic as well as mouse models of MLL-rearranged leukemia and becomes hallmarks of this malignancy. DOT1L therefore represents a drug target for MLL leukemias. Indeed, this has been established by biological means (e.g., knockdown by RNA interference) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR66" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR66">66</a></span>] and pharmacological inhibition as described below [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR68" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR68">68</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR76" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR76">76</a></span>].<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig4"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO4">
                      <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig4_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig4_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                    </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 4</span>
                        <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Functions of wild-type MLL, LSD1 and onco-MLL fusion proteins. <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">a</strong> MLL methylates H3K4 and initiates RNA polymerase II (Pol II) mediated gene transcription, while LSD1 removes the methyl group from H3K4me1 and 2 and keeps a balanced H3K4 methylation; <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">b</strong> The onco-MLL protein complex involving AF4, AF9, AF10, or ENL can recruit DOT1L, which methylates H3K79 and causes overexpression of leukemia-relevant genes</p>
                      </div></figcaption></figure>
                        </div>
              </section><section id="Sec5" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">DOT1L inhibitors and their activity against MLL leukemia</h3>
                <div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par13" class="Para">Because of DOT1L’s crucial role in oncogenesis and maintenance of MLL-rearranged leukemia, much effort has been dedicated to find small molecule inhibitors of DOT1L. The first DOT1L inhibitor EPZ004777 (1, Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig5">5</a></span>) as well as its selective antitumor activity against MLL leukemia were reported in 2011 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR68" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR68">68</a></span>]. Several other potent DOT1L inhibitors were disclosed shortly after [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR69" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR69">69</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR75" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR75">75</a></span>]. Figure.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig5">5</a></span> summarizes representative inhibitors of DOT1L, as well as their biochemical and antitumor activities. It is noted that all currently disclosed DOT1L inhibitors contain an adenosine or its analogous structure and are competitive to the enzyme cofactor SAM. This is likely because of the difficulty to compete with the substrate nucleosome, which has strong protein-protein and protein-DNA interactions with DOT1L [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR70" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR70">70</a></span>].<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig5"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO5">
                      <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig5_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig5_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                    </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 5</span>
                        <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Structures and activities of representative DOT1L inhibitors</p>
                      </div></figcaption></figure>
                        </div>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par14" class="Para">The discovery of compound 1 followed a conventional ligand-based medicinal chemistry approach [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR69" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR69">69</a></span>]. Starting with the natural inhibitor SAH (<em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">K</em>
                           <sub>i</sub> = 260 nM), a series of repeated cycles of chemical modifications followed by structure activity relationship studies yielded 1, which still has an adenosine-like moiety, while the sidechain in SAH is replaced with a tert-butylphenyl urea containing tertiary amine group. Compound 1 exhibited an extremely potent inhibitory activity against DOT1L with a <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">K</em>
                           <sub>i</sub> value of 0.3 nM, ~860-fold more active than SAH. In addition, unlike SAH being a broadly active inhibitor of HMTs, 1 shows an excellent enzyme selectivity profile: it did not inhibit a panel of 8 other HMTs at 50&nbsp;μM, although 1 is a relatively weak inhibitor of PRMT5 (IC<sub>50</sub>: 520 nM). X-ray crystallographic studies showed that the binding of compound 1 causes large protein conformational changes of DOT1L to accommodate the large hydrophobic sidechain [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR69" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR69">69</a></span>]. Surface plasmon resonance (SPR) studies revealed that a very slow <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">k</em>
                           <sub>off</sub> rate mostly accounts for the potent inhibitory activity of compound 1. Further inhibitor optimization produced EPZ-5676 (<strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">2</strong>) with an improved activity (<em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">K</em>
                           <sub>i</sub> = 0.08 nM) as well as cellular activities [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR73" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR73">73</a></span>]. Several other groups also disclosed their DOT1L inhibitors. Structure-based design led to the finding of compound 3, a N6-methyl substituted SAH, which retains the inhibitory activity of SAH against DOT1L and exhibits excellent selectivity against other HMTs [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR71" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR71">71</a></span>]. Compound 4 is a potent, mechanism-based inhibitor of DOT1L, with the ability to covalently bind to the substrate [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR71" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR71">71</a></span>]. Compounds 5 and 6 are analogs of 1 and also showed highly potent inhibition against DOT1L (<em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">K</em>
                           <sub>i</sub> = 1.1 and 0.06 nM) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR75" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR75">75</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR76" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR76">76</a></span>]. Compound 5 with a cyclopentane ring (rather than the ribose in other compounds) was designed, synthesized and shown to retain a potent inhibitory activity against DOT1L. However, it exhibited a significantly improved metabolic stability [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR75" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR75">75</a></span>]. X-ray structure-based design for compound 6 with a 7-bromo substituent on the adenine ring was intended to exploit a nearby hydrophobic pocket and it turned out that 6 is one of the most potent inhibitors of DOT1L [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR76" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR76">76</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par15" class="Para">Compounds 1, 2, 5, and 6 are cell permeable and exhibit selective activities against MLL-rearranged leukemia cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR68" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR68">68</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR73" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR73">73</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR75" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR75">75</a></span>]. It is remarkable that these DOT1L inhibitors are not cytotoxic and did not exhibit anti-proliferative activity for a short (e.g., 3&nbsp;days) treatment. Rather, a long time (e.g., 14&nbsp;days) incubation is needed, showing a distinct mechanism of action for these compounds [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR69" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR69">69</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR70" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR70">70</a></span>]. The slow action is likely because of a long time required for a series of cellular events that arrest cell growth, including H3K79 methylation inhibition, followed by systematic changes of gene expression. Compound 1 only inhibited cellular H3K79 methylation with an IC<sub>50</sub> of ~50 nM and did not affect other histone methylations significantly. 1 inhibited the proliferation of MLL-rearranged leukemia cells with EC<sub>50</sub> values of 0.17–6.5&nbsp;μM, while it showed significantly reduced activity against leukemia cells without a MLL rearrangement (EC<sub>50</sub>: 13.9–&gt;50&nbsp;μM). Treatment with 1 caused downregulation of onco-MLL target genes and induced cell differentiation as well as apoptosis of MLL-rearranged leukemia cells. Gene profiling also revealed that there were significant overlaps in gene expression pattern between samples treated with compound <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">1</strong> and DOT1L knockdown, supporting DOT1L is the cellular drug target. Due to the poor pharmacokinetics, continuous infusion using an osmotic pump was chosen for administration of compound <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">1</strong> for in vivo studies. A dose of 70&nbsp;mg/kg for 21&nbsp;days was able to cause a regression of subcutaneous tumors of MV4-11 leukemia cells. In a more clinically relevant systemic MV4-11 leukemia mouse model, compound <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">1</strong> can also prolong survival of the experimental animals with statistical significance.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par16" class="Para">Compound 2 is the most potent DOT1L inhibitor, together with improved pharmacokinetics [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR73" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR73">73</a></span>]. It showed more potent cellular activities (e.g., EC<sub>50</sub>s = 0.004–1.5&nbsp;μM) and in vivo antitumor efficacy. It has been in phase I clinical trials against MLL-rearranged leukemia. Preliminary clinical results of compound 2 were disclosed in the 56<sup>th</sup> Annual Meeting of American Society of Hematology [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR77" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR77">77</a></span>] as well as several press releases (<span class="ExternalRef"><a href="http://www.epizyme.com/"><span class="RefSource">www.epizyme.com</span></a></span>). A total of 37 advanced leukemia patients, who were heavily pretreated with chemotherapies, were enrolled and received 6 doses (ranging from 12 to 90&nbsp;mg/m<sup>2</sup>/day for 21 or 28&nbsp;days) of continuous infusion of 2. The compound was well tolerated, with the main adverse events being grade 1 or 2 leukocytosis, nausea and hypomagnesemia. Drug administration can achieve a rapid steady-state plasma concentration of compound 2 on day-1 and cause inhibition of H3K79 methylation in patients’ bone marrow as well as peripheral blood cells. Eight patients out of 34 with an MLL-translocation showed biological or clinical activity, among whom two complete responses as well as one partial response were observed. More clinical trials including combination therapies with other drugs are being conducted.</p>
              </section><section id="Sec6" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">DOT1L in other diseases</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par17" class="Para">DOT1L has been identified to be a drug target for several other types of cancers [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR78" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR78">78</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR80" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR80">80</a></span>]. A bioinformatics search found the expression levels of DOT1L correlate with breast cancer as well as a panel of genes that promote proliferation of the malignancy [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR78" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR78">78</a></span>]. Knockdown of DOT1L and pharmacological inhibition (by e.g., compound 1) showed inhibition of H3K79 methylation and cell proliferation of several DOT1L<sup>+</sup> breast cancer cell lines with EC<sub>50</sub> of 0.19–1.4&nbsp;μM, while DOT1L<sup>low</sup> breast cancer cells were not sensitive to DOT1L inhibition. Mechanistically, inhibition of DOT1L/H3K79 methylation can impair self-renewal and metastatic potential, induce differentiation and down-regulate many pro-proliferation genes, all of which contribute to significantly reduced proliferation of these breast cancer cells. H3K79 hypermethylation was observed for lung cancer cell lines A549 and NCI-H1299. siRNA-mediated DOT1L knockdown can block the proliferation of these cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR79" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR79">79</a></span>]. Therefore, these experiments implied that DOT1L plays an important role in lung cancer. However, no DOT1L inhibitors were tested to confirm this finding. In addition to cancer, DOT1L was recently found to play a role in cell reprogramming. DOT1L knockdown as well as compound 1 were shown to significantly increase the reprogramming efficiency of somatic cells to produce more induced pluripotent stem cells (iPSC), showing the potential of using DOT1L inhibitors in regenerative medicine [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR80" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR80">80</a></span>].</p>
              </section></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec7" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">MLL and LSD1 modifying H3K4 methylation</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><section id="Sec8" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">Cancer biology of MLL and LSD1</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par18" class="Para">The biology of MLL has been extensively studies and reviewed [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR52" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR52">52</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR54" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR54">54</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR60" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR60">60</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR62" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR62">62</a></span>] and briefly summarized in the above section and here. The biological function of MLL is essential for development: knockout of MLL in mice is embryonic lethal. MLL has been found to associate with thousands of gene promoters and have a global role in positive regulation of transcription of many important genes such as Hox families of genes [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR81" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR81">81</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR82" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR82">82</a></span>]. Hox genes are transcriptional factors essential for the development of multiple tissues including the hematopoietic system, while overexpression of certain members (e.g., HoxA9 etc.) has been found to lead to leukemogenesis [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR83" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR83">83</a></span>]. MLL gene translocations are frequently found in acute leukemia. Moreover, it is noted that MLL-translocation occurs in one allele, with the wild-type (WT) MLL in the other allele remaining intact. A recent study showed that MLL’s H3K4 methyltransferase activity is essential for MLL-rearranged leukemia, suggesting inhibition of the SET domain of MLL is a possibly viable approach to MLL leukemia treatment [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR84" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR84">84</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par19" class="Para">LSD1 plays an opposite role as a histone lysine demethylase [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR22" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR22">22</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR23" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR23">23</a></span>]. LSD1 contains four functional domains, including an N-terminal domain with a putative nuclear localization peptide, a SWIRM, and an oxidase domain, inside which there is a tower domain insert [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR85" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR85">85</a></span>]. The last three domains are important for demethylation. In addition, the tower domain, which is not present in a closely related enzyme LSD2, directly interacts with CoREST (also known as RCOR1, repressor element-1 silencing transcription factor corepressor 1), through which LSD1 forms protein complexes that regulate histone lysine methylation as well as gene expression. The biological function of LSD1 is crucial, as germline LSD1 knockout in mice was found to be embryonic lethal and conditional knockout caused increased H3K4me1/2, blocked hematopoiesis and pancytopenia (low in all blood cell types) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR86" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR86">86</a></span>]. The primary substrates of LSD1 are H3K4me1 and me2, which are important histone marks for active gene transcription. LSD1 was found to be part of an MLL transcription complex [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR87" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR87">87</a></span>]. A possible function of LSD1 is to counteract MLL and keep a balanced H3K4 methylation (Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig4">4a</a></span>). Of interest is that LSD1 has recently been found to be required for leukemia stem cells transformed with MLL-AF9 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR88" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR88">88</a></span>]. LSD1 knockdown abrogated the transforming ability of MLL-AF9, increased the H3K4me2 levels at MLL-AF9 target gene loci, and reduced the expression of HoxA9 and Meis1. Presumably, LSD1 inhibition could counteract the loss of the SET domain in MLL-AF9 and restore a balanced H3K4 methylation. Pharmacological inhibition of LSD1 showed similar activities against MLL-AF9 leukemia in vitro and in vivo [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR88" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR88">88</a></span>]. Although there is a safety concern of LSD1 inhibition [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR89" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR89">89</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR90" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR90">90</a></span>] because of LSD1’s role in hematopoiesis [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR86" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR86">86</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR91" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR91">91</a></span>], a recent study showed after termination of LSD1 conditional knockout the impaired hematopoiesis can be recovered in a mouse model [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR91" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR91">91</a></span>]. These lines of evidence strongly support that LSD1 is a drug target for MLL leukemia.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par20" class="Para">In addition, H3K9 and other proteins have been found to be LSD1’s substrates [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR92" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR92">92</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR96" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR96">96</a></span>]. In the context of androgen receptor-mediated gene expression, histone H3 threonine 6 is phosphorylated by PKCβ1, which prevents LSD1 from binding to methylated H3K4. In complex with androgen receptor and PKCβ1, LSD1 can change its substrate-specificity and demethylate H3K9me1 and 2 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR92" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR92">92</a></span>]. DNA methyltransferase 1 (DNMT1), which maintains the integrity of DNA methylation as well as plays an important role in maintaining hematopoietic stem and progenitor cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR97" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR97">97</a></span>], is also a substrate of LSD1. DNMT1 is methylated in vivo and such methylation destabilizes the protein. LSD1 can demethylate and therefore stabilize DNMT1. Therefore, LSD1 is of importance in maintaining global DNA methylation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR93" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR93">93</a></span>]. In addition, LSD1 can demethylate other non-histone proteins, such as p53 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR94" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR94">94</a></span>], MYPT1 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR95" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR95">95</a></span>], and STAT3 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR96" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR96">96</a></span>], and regulate gene expression mediated by these proteins.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par21" class="Para">Overexpression of LSD1 has been found in many types of cancer [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR98" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR98">98</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR102" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR102">102</a></span>], including AML (without an MLL-translocation), lung, breast, and prostate cancer. These observations implicate that LSD1 is a potential drug target for these tumors. It was recently found that a significant portion of cell lines of AML and small cell lung cancer (SCLC) are highly sensitive to pharmacological inhibition of LSD1 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR103" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR103">103</a></span>]. Although except for MLL-rearranged leukemia, the molecular mechanisms that link LSD1 to these malignancies are not fully understood (likely because LSD1 has multiple protein substrates), inhibition of LSD1 generally caused broad gene expression pattern changes in these sensitive tumors, which could be responsible for the anti-proliferative activity and other effects, e.g., inducing apoptosis and/or differentiation.</p>
              </section><section id="Sec9" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">LSD1 inhibitors and their biological activities</h3>
                <div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par22" class="Para">A number of small molecule inhibitors of LSD1 have been discovered, developed, reported in the journals and patents [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR103" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR103">103</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR112" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR112">112</a></span>] and reviewed recently [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR16" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR16">16</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR113" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR113">113</a></span>]. These compounds can be classified into reversible and irreversible inhibitors depending upon their modes of action. We focus on the biological activities of the most potent compounds. Figure&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig6">6</a></span> summarizes representative inhibitors of these two classes, together with their enzyme and cellular activities. LSD1 belongs to a family of monoamine oxidases (MAO), using FAD as the cofactor for the redox reaction (Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig2">2a</a></span>). The common feature for irreversible LSD1 inhibitors is that upon oxidation, part of the molecules is able to covalently bind to FAD and permanently deactivates the enzyme [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR22" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR22">22</a></span>]. However, for reversible inhibitors, there is no covalent interaction between the inhibitor and the protein.<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig6"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO6">
                      <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig6_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig6_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                    </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 6</span>
                        <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Structures and activities of representative LSD1 inhibitors</p>
                      </div></figcaption></figure>
                        </div>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par23" class="Para">The first inhibitors of LSD1 with a common cyclopropylamine core structure were derived from Tranylcypromine (7, Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig6">6</a></span>), an FDA-approved antidepression drug. Compound 7 is an inhibitor of MAO-A and -B, enzymes that degrade neurotransmitters in the central nervous system. Compound 7 weakly inhibits LSD1 with an IC<sub>50</sub> of ~15&nbsp;μM. More potent inhibitors have been developed based on the structure of 7. Of importance for the inhibitor optimization is the introduction of a second amine-containing N-substituent, such as those on the right side of cyclopropylamine moiety in highly potent LSD1 inhibitors 8–10. These basic groups not only greatly increase the inhibitory activity, but also render excellent LSD1 selectivity against MAO-A and -B [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR112" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR112">112</a></span>]. Compound <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">8</strong> (compound B in [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR88" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR88">88</a></span>]) inhibited recombinant LSD1 in vitro with an IC<sub>50</sub> of 98 nM. It showed antitumor activities against MLL-AF9 transformed leukemic stem cells. It inhibited colony-forming ability of MLL-AF9 containing leukemia cells with EC<sub>50</sub> values as low as 50 nM. It also down-regulated expression of many leukemia-relevant genes such as HoxA7, HoxA9 and Meis1. However, it exhibited somewhat severe toxicities in a mouse model of MLL-AF9 leukemia, causing deaths of many experimental mice presumably due to insufficient inhibitory potency and/or inappropriate dosages. Compound 9 (compound 1 in [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR111" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR111">111</a></span>]) is a much more potent LSD1 inhibitor, almost quantitatively deactivating the enzyme with an IC<sub>50</sub> of 9.8 nM. It exhibited potent anti-proliferative activity against MLL-rearranged leukemia cell lines MV4-11 and Molm-13 with EC<sub>50</sub> values of 10 and 96 nM, while 9 is almost inactive against leukemia cells NB4 and U937 without an MLL-translocation. The differential activities of compound 9 (as well as several other LSD1 inhibitors) suggest that the LSD1 inhibitor is non-cytotoxic, but LSD1 is essential for MLL-rearranged leukemia cells. In vivo antitumor studies using a systemic murine model of MV4-11 leukemia showed that compound 9 did not exhibit overt toxicities and was able to inhibit leukemia progression by &gt;90&nbsp;% and significantly prolong survival of the experimental animals. Another potent LSD1 inhibitor GSK2879552 (10) was found to exhibit high anti-proliferative activity against 20 out of 29 AML cell lines with EC<sub>50</sub> values ranging from ~3–100 nM [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR103" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR103">103</a></span>]. In addition, anti-proliferation screening of compound 10 led to the finding that a significant portion (9 out of 28) of small cell lung carcinoma (SCLC) cell lines were susceptible to 10 with EC<sub>50</sub>s of ~2–240 nM. This compound also showed significant antitumor activity in a mouse xenograft model of SCLC cancer. Similarly, compound <strong xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeBold">10</strong> is also devoid of general cytotoxicity: it did not inhibit the growth of &gt;100 cell lines across a range of cancer types, showing a high selectivity of using LSD1 inhibitors targeting cancer. Mechanistic studies showed that gene expression of TGF-β signaling, which is dysregulated in SCLC, was significantly altered upon treatment with compound 10. This could be attributed to the antitumor activity of the LSD1 inhibitor. In addition, DNA hypomethylation of a gene set was identified to be correlated with the sensitivity of SCLC cells (including primary patient samples) to LSD1 inhibition [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR105" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR105">105</a></span>]. This biomarker could be used as a major criterion for patient recruitment. Compound 10 has currently been in clinical trials for SCLC, while no clinical data have been disclosed.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par24" class="Para">Several chemo-types of reversible LSD1 inhibitors have been disclosed, among which compounds SP2509 (11) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR107" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR107">107</a></span>], GSK690 (12) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR16" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR16">16</a></span>], and 13 (compound 17 in [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR112" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR112">112</a></span>]) possess low nM in vitro inhibitory activity. Compound 11 potently inhibited LSD1 with an IC<sub>50</sub> of 13 nM, showing a non-competitive mode of action. Treatment with 11 increased promoter-specific H3K4 methylation, inhibited colony-formation, and induced differentiation and apoptosis of several AML cell lines including MV4-11, Molm-13, and OCI-AML3. The combination of 11 with an inhibitor of HDAC exhibited synergy and showed significantly improved in vivo antileukemia activity in mouse models of AML [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR107" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR107">107</a></span>]. Compounds 12 and 13 are quite similar, with the same 3-, 5-, 6-trisubstituted pyridine core structure. Preliminary biological data of compound 12 were presented in the 2013 American Association of Cancer Research annual meeting, showing 90 nM IC<sub>50</sub> against LSD1, high enzyme selectivity, as well as low μM cellular activity against AML cells. Compound 13 showed an improved in vitro inhibitory activity (29 nM) against LSD1 as well as good anti-proliferative activities (EC<sub>50</sub>: 0.36–3.6&nbsp;μM) against several sensitive cancer cells including MV4-11 with MLL-AF4 oncogene [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR112" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR112">112</a></span>].</p>
              </section><section id="Sec10" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">MLL inhibitors</h3>
                <div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par25" class="Para">In MLL-rearranged leukemia, the MLL gene translocation is heterozygous. The H3K4 methyltransferase activity of the remaining copy of WT MLL was found to be essential for the malignancy [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR92" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR92">92</a></span>]. Therefore, MLL inhibitors could be useful to treat MLL-rearranged leukemia. However, compounds that directly inhibit the SET domain of MLL have not been reported. Alternatively, the MLL SET domain alone was found to have extremely low methyltransferase activity [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR114" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR114">114</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR115" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR115">115</a></span>]. The optimal enzyme activity requires its complexation with three other proteins, i.e., WDR5, ASH2L, and RbBP5. Among these, the interaction between WDR5 and MLL is critical, which led to the finding of indirect MLL inhibitors, compounds that disrupt the binding of WDR5 to MLL [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR116" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR116">116</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR117" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR117">117</a></span>]. Several compounds have recently been found to bind to WDR5 with <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">K</em>
                           <sub>d</sub> values of &lt;0.001–5.5&nbsp;μM. To date, the best compound MM-401 (14, Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig7">7</a></span>), an extremely tight binder to WDR5 with a <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">K</em>
                           <sub>d</sub> value of &lt;1 nM, showed an IC<sub>50</sub> of 0.9 nM in disrupting the interaction between WDR5 and MLL. Indeed, compound 14 almost quantitatively inhibited the methyltransferase activity of the MLL complex (0.5&nbsp;μM) with an IC<sub>50</sub> value of 0.32&nbsp;μM in vitro. Because the WDR5-MLL interaction is unique, compound 14 exhibited a high enzyme selectivity profile: it did not inhibit several closely related SET domain methyltransferases including MLL2 - 4, SET1, and SET7/9. Consistent with MLL’s role, it showed selective activity against MLL-rearranged leukemia cells. Compound 14 inhibited the proliferation of MLL-rearranged leukemia cells with EC<sub>50</sub> values of 12–30&nbsp;μM, while it had no effects on other non-MLL leukemia cells. The relatively weak cellular activity might be due to the poor cell permeability of compound 14, a cyclic peptidomimetic compound.<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig7"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO7">
                      <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig7_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig7_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                    </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 7</span>
                        <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Structure and activity of a compound that disrupts MLL:WDR5 interactions and thereby inhibits MLL indirectly</p>
                      </div></figcaption></figure>
                        </div>
              </section></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec11" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">H3K27 methyltransferase EZH2</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><section id="Sec12" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">H3K27 methylation and EZH2 in health and cancer</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par26" class="Para">SET domain containing EZH2 (Enhancer-of-zeste homolog 2) and its close homolog EZH1 catalyze mono-, di- and tri-methylation of H3K27 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR118" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR118">118</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR120" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR120">120</a></span>]. EZH2 was originally identified as a member protein of the Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2), which functions as a transcription repressor of Hox gene clusters important to the development [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR121" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR121">121</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR123" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR123">123</a></span>]. In 2002, it was further determined to be a methyltransferase of H3K27 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR118" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR118">118</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR120" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR120">120</a></span>]. EZH2 alone is not catalytically active. Complexation with EED and SUZ12, two other members in PRC2, is required to methylate H3K27 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR124" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR124">124</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR125" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR125">125</a></span>]. The biology of EZH2-containing PRC2 as well as their mediated H3K27 methylation has been reviewed [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR121" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR121">121</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR123" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR123">123</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR126" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR126">126</a></span>]. Briefly, EZH2 is essential for embryonic development and plays important roles in normal physiology. EZH2 mediated H3K27me3 has been found to be a “histone code” for transcription repression, while the genes silenced by EZH2/H3K27me3 are different, depending upon cell types [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR127" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR127">127</a></span>]. Such epigenetic transcription repression is essential to cell fate determination and function. However, the underlying regulatory mechanism for the gene silencing is complicated and not fully understood. Other proteins in the PRC2 complex also contribute to the gene silencing. For example, studies showed that DNMT is associated with EZH2 in PRC2 and EZH2 is required to recruit DNMT to its target gene promoters and methylate the DNA [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR128" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR128">128</a></span>]. This finding showed the two epigenetic events, i.e., H3K27me3 and DNA methylation, are interconnected for transcription repression. In addition, although the homologous EZH1 also catalyzes H3K27 methylation as well as associates with PRC2, it has different biological functions and cannot substitute EZH2 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR129" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR129">129</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par27" class="Para">Overexpression of EZH2 is often found in a variety of cancers such as breast, prostate, lung and blood cancers [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR130" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR130">130</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR135" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR135">135</a></span>], with its expression level correlated with that of H3K27me3, more virulent progression of the disease, and poor prognosis. Further studies suggested that EZH2 plays important roles in tumorigenesis, progression as well as metastasis [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR136" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR136">136</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR137" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR137">137</a></span>]. However, it remains to be answered in these cases whether the methyltransferase activity of EZH2 is required for these cancers. Using potent and selective EZH2 inhibitors, studies have recently shown that proliferation of several types of cancer cells was not significantly affected by pharmacological inhibition of EZH2/H3K27 methylation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR78" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR78">78</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR138" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR138">138</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR139" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR139">139</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par28" class="Para">Recently, somatic mutations of EZH2 at Y641, and A677 were identified in 12–25&nbsp;% non-Hodgkin lymphomas [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR140" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR140">140</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR142" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR142">142</a></span>]. Biochemical investigation revealed that these EHZ2 mutants exhibit a different substrate-specificity from that of the WT enzyme [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR143" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR143">143</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR145" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR145">145</a></span>]. The WT EZH2 catalyzes the mono-methylation of H3K27 with the highest speed, while it is significantly less efficient for the second and especially third methylation of H3K27. A reversed trend was observed for the Y641 mutant proteins: they are inactive for the first methylation, but these mutants transfer the second and third methyl to H3K27 in an increasing catalytic efficiency. In addition, the A677G mutant EZH2 can catalyze all three methylation at a high speed. Clinically, these EZH2 mutated lymphomas exhibit a particularly high levels of H3K27me3.</p>
              </section><section id="Sec13" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">EZH2 inhibitors</h3>
                <div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par29" class="Para">Recent high-throughput screening campaigns followed by medicinal chemistry have identified several potent and highly selective inhibitors of EZH2, as representatively shown in Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig8">8</a></span>. GSK126 (15) is the first disclosed inhibitor with a <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">K</em>
                           <sub>i</sub> value of 0.5–3 nM against WT and all mutant EZH2 enzymes [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR139" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR139">139</a></span>]. It showed &gt;150-fold selectivity against EZH1 (<em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">K</em>
                           <sub>i</sub> = 89 nM), as well as &gt;1000-fold selectivity against other HMTs. Enzyme kinetics studies suggested that compound 15 is competitive with the enzyme cofactor SAM and non-competitive with the substrate H3K27. It can potently inhibit cellular H3K27me3 levels with IC<sub>50</sub> values of 7–252 nM in a broad variety of cell lines, independent upon the status of EZH2 mutation. Compound 15 was found to have strong anti-proliferative activities (EC<sub>50</sub> = 28–861 nM) against diffuse large B-cell lymphoma cells containing an EZH2 activating mutation, while lymphoma cells without an EZH2 mutation are generally insensitive. The antitumor activity has also been confirmed in a mouse model study. Treatment with compound 15 was able to cause the xenograft tumors to regress in a dose-dependent manner and significantly prolong the survivals of the tumor bearing mice.
