Mechanistically, treatment with compound 15 reduced the cellular H3K27me3 levels and caused a broad range of transcriptional activation of EZH2/PRC2 target genes in sensitive lymphoma cells, while it did not significantly affect the gene expression patterns of insensitive cells.<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig8"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO8">
                      <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig8_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig8_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                    </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 8</span>
                        <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Structures and activities of representative EZH2 inhibitors</p>
                      </div></figcaption></figure>
                        </div>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par30" class="Para">Scientists from several other institutes have also reported their potent EZH2 inhibitors with enzyme IC<sub>50</sub> values in the very low nM range [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR138" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR138">138</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR146" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR146">146</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR149" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR149">149</a></span>], as shown representatively in Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig8">8</a></span>. Most of these potent EZH2 inhibitors contain the same or similar N-(2-pyridinone-3-yl)methyl amide substructure, suggesting this moiety is important for protein binding. Enzyme kinetics studies showed that these compounds exhibited a competitive mode of action against the cofactor SAM, suggesting that these compounds bind to the SAM-binding site in EZH2. However, no X-ray structures of EZH2-inhibitor complexes have been reported to date. The exact binding modes of these compounds still need to be explored, which could provide useful structural information for further rational inhibitor design targeting EZH2. Recently, EZH2 inhibitors 16 and CPI-1205 (with an undisclosed structure) have entered clinical trials against EZH2 mutated non-Hodgkin lymphomas, while the results have not been disclosed.</p>
              </section></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec14" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Isocitrate dehydrogenase (IDH) mutation and histone methylation</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><section id="Sec15" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">IDH mutation in glioma and other cancers</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par31" class="Para">Isocitrate dehydrogenase (IDH) is one of the key enzymes in the tricarboxylic acid cycle for aerobic metabolism of glucose [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR150" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR150">150</a></span>]. There are three IDH isozymes in humans, with IDH1 located in cytoplasm and IDH2 and 3 in mitochondria [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR151" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR151">151</a></span>]. Recent genetic studies have identified recurrent mutations of IDH in a number of cancers. IDH1 mutations were found in ~75&nbsp;% low-grade gliomas as well as secondary glioblastoma multiforme (GBM), the grade IV glioma developed from the low-grade tumors [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR152" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR152">152</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR156" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR156">156</a></span>]. Of particular interest is that all these mutations occur exclusively in the R132 residue of IDH1 or the corresponding R172 residue of IDH2, with the R132H mutation being predominant (&gt;90&nbsp;%) in these gliomas. Mutations of IDH1 or 2 have also been found in ~20&nbsp;% AML and several subtypes of sarcomas [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR157" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR157">157</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR160" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR160">160</a></span>]. The IDH mutations occur at an early stage of these cancers, suggesting they could play an important role in oncogenesis [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR161" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR161">161</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR163" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR163">163</a></span>].</p>
                <div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par32" class="Para">Biochemically, although mutant and WT IDH enzymes do not directly work on histones, the products of their catalyzed reactions are an inhibitor or cofactor of JmjC domain histone demethylases (Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig2">2c</a></span>) and therefore affect histone lysine methylation. As shown in Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig9">9a</a></span>, WT IDH catalyzes the oxidative decarboxylation of isocitric acid to produce α-ketoglutaric acid (α-KG) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR150" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR150">150</a></span>], while mutant IDH enzymes lose this function [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR164" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR164">164</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR165" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR165">165</a></span>]. Rather, all these mutants catalyze a new reaction, the reduction of α-KG to <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">D</em>-2-hydroxyglutaric acid (D2HG) (Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig9">9b</a></span>) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR157" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR157">157</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR164" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR164">164</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR166" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR166">166</a></span>]. This new function generates &gt;100-fold elevated concentrations of D2HG in tumor cells bearing an IDH1 or IDH2 mutation, while there is only trace amount of D2HG in normal cells. The extremely high level of D2HG is therefore a hallmark of IDH mutated glioma and is suggested to be used as a biomarker for these types of cancers [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR157" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR157">157</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR164" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR164">164</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR166" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR166">166</a></span>]. D2HG with its structure being very similar to α-KG has been found to inhibit α-KG-dependent KDMs and TET family of 5-methylcytosine hydroxylases [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR159" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR159">159</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR167" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR167">167</a></span>], which are important enzymes to maintain a balanced methylation status of histone and DNA. IDH mutated cancers exhibited genome-wide hypermethylation of histone and DNA [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR159" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR159">159</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR167" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR167">167</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR169" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR169">169</a></span>]. In addition, transfection of IDH1 R132H mutation into U87 GBM cells without an IDH mutation blocked cell differentiation and also caused DNA/histone hypermethylation in a very similar fashion to that observed in the clinical tumor samples [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR168" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR168">168</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR170" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR170">170</a></span>]. These lines of evidence strongly support IDH mutation is a drug target for intervention.<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig9"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO9">
                      <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig9_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig9_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                    </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 9</span>
                        <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Mechanisms and inhibitors of IDH. <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">a</strong> Mechanism of catalysis for the wild-type IDH. <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">b</strong> Mechanism of catalysis for mutant IDHs. <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">c</strong> Structures and activities of representative inhibitors of mutant IDH</p>
                      </div></figcaption></figure>
                        </div>
              </section></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec16" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Inhibitors of mutant IDH</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par33" class="Para">Significant efforts from the academia and pharmaceutical industry have been given to discover small molecule inhibitors of mutant IDH. A number of potent and selective inhibitors of mutant IDH1 and 2 with several chemo-types have been published [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR171" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR171">171</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR177" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR177">177</a></span>] and Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig9">9c</a></span> summarizes several representative compounds together with their biological activities.</p><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par34" class="Para">AGI-5198 (18) and AGI-6780 (19) with distinct chemical structures were the first inhibitors of mutant IDH1 and IDH2, respectively [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR172" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR172">172</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR173" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR173">173</a></span>]. Dipeptide-like compound 18 exhibited potent inhibitory activity against IDH1 R132H (IC<sub>50</sub> = 70 nM) and R132C (IC<sub>50</sub> = 160 nM) mutants, respectively, while it did not inhibit WT IDH1 or WT and mutant IDH2 enzymes (IC<sub>50</sub> &gt; 100&nbsp;μM). This compound can also reduce cellular D2HG concentrations and the colony-forming ability of glioma cells with IDH1 R132H mutation. Because of the reduced cellular D2HG as well as restored activity of histone demethylases, H3K9 methylation, a repressive histone mark, was found to be decreased globally as well as in several gene promoters. Compound 18 exhibited in vivo antitumor activity in a mouse xenograft model of IDH1 mutated glioma. Compound 19 is a urea-substituted benzenesulfamide compound, showing selective activity against various mutant forms of IDH2 with IC<sub>50</sub> values of 4 – 170 nM, while it exhibited high selectivity against WT IDH2 or IDH1 enzymes. It can similarly reduce cellular D2HG concentrations effectively, inhibit proliferation and colony-forming ability, and promote differentiation of primary AML cells harboring an IDH2 mutation. Compounds 20–22 were reported as potent and selective inhibitors of mutant IDH1, showing 0.013–0.42&nbsp;μM inhibitory activities [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR174" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR174">174</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR177" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR177">177</a></span>]. Compounds 21 and 22 can also selectively impair colony-forming ability of patient derived glioma cells having R132H IDH1 mutation.</p><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par35" class="Para">AG-221 (23), an advanced inhibitor of mutant IDH2, has been in phase I clinical trials against AML or myelodysplastic syndrome with IDH2 mutations. Based on data disclosed in the company’s press releases (<span class="ExternalRef"><a href="http://www.agios.com/"><span class="RefSource">www.agios.com</span></a></span>), compound 23 exhibited promising clinical pharmacokinetic, safety and efficacy profiles. A total of 177 patients, who have relapsed or refractory AML with IDH2 mutations, have been treated with various doses of compound 23. The drug seems to be well tolerated, with the majority of adverse effects being mild to moderate, including nausea, fatigue, increased blood bilirubin and diarrhea. The maximum tolerated dose of 23 has not been reached. Preliminary pharmacokinetic and pharmacodynamics studies showed compound 23 has an excellent oral availability, and a high plasma half-life of &gt;40&nbsp;h. Objective responses have been observed for 63 patients out of 158 evaluable patients, showing an overall response rate of 40&nbsp;%. Among these, there were 26 cases of complete remission (CR), three cases of CR with incomplete platelet recovery, 14 cases of marrow CR, two cases of CR with incomplete hematological recovery as well as 18 cases of partial remission. Given these promising clinical data, more clinical evaluations of compound 23 as well as several other inhibitors of mutant IDH are currently on-going worldwide.</p></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec17" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Inhibitors of selected other histone methylation enzymes</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><div xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par36" class="Para">There are excellent review articles that summarize the biology of HKMTs, PRMTs and KDMs as well as their relevance to cancer or other diseases [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR4" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR4">4</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR8" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR8">8</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR17" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR17">17</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR178" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR178">178</a></span>]. As compared to the large number (&gt;100) of these histone methylation enzymes, relatively fewer number of potent and selective small molecule inhibitors have been discovered in the past few years. We describe below potent inhibitors of several other important histone methylation modifying enzymes (Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig10">10</a></span>). Most of these compounds are cell permeable and can be used as chemical probes for biological studies of these proteins. However, a number of previously reported inhibitors are not included, because of low potency (e.g., with μM enzyme IC<sub>50</sub>s), non-specificity, or unknown mechanism of action. It is therefore unclear whether the observed biological activities of these compounds are due to inhibition of the protein target or off-target effects.<figure xmlns="" class="Figure" id="Fig10"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="MediaObject" id="MO10">
                    <a target="_blank" href="http://media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig10_HTML.gif"><img src="http://media.springernature.com/lw785/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13045-016-0279-9/MediaObjects/13045_2016_279_Fig10_HTML.gif"></img></a>
                  </div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Fig. 10</span>
                      <p xmlns="" class="SimplePara">Structures and activities of representative inhibitors of <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">a</strong> G9a/GLP; <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">b</strong> SMYD2; <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">c</strong> PRMTs; and <strong class="EmphasisTypeBold">d</strong> KDM6</p>
                    </div></figcaption></figure>
                     </div><section id="Sec18" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">Inhibitors of other HKMTs</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par37" class="Para">G9a (also known as EHMT2) and its closely homologous protein GLP (also known as EHMT1) are responsible for mono- and di-methylation of H3K9 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR179" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR179">179</a></span>] and play important roles in gene regulation. There is also strong evidence that G9a/GLP is implicated in several types of cancers [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR180" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR180">180</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR182" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR182">182</a></span>]. The first specific inhibitor of G9a is BIX-01294 (24, Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig10">10a</a></span>) identified in a high-throughput screening [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR183" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR183">183</a></span>]. Compound 24 exhibits an IC<sub>50</sub> of 180 nM against G9α, whereas no inhibition of the other histone methyltransferases, such as SUV39H1 and PRMT1, was observed. In a cellular assay, 24 was found to transiently modulate H3K9me2 marks. Medicinal chemistry modifications based on the scaffold of 24 yielded a series of compounds with improved potency as well as cell permeability, such as UNC0224 (25) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR21" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR21">21</a></span>] and UNC0638 (26) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR184" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR184">184</a></span>]. Compound 25 exhibited an improved IC<sub>50</sub> of 43 nM against G9α by adding a lysine mimetic group. An X-ray crystal structure of G9a in complex with 25 revealed that this group occupies the lysine binding channel of the protein. Compound 26 is a potent, selective and cell permeable G9a inhibitor, able to reduce H3K9me2 levels in a dose-dependent manner with an EC<sub>50</sub> of 81 nM. Furthermore, compound 26 can reduce colony-forming ability of breast cancer MCF-7 and MDA-MB231 cells. Compound A-366 (27) with a distinct chemo-type is also a highly potent and selective inhibitor of G9a with an IC<sub>50</sub> of 3.3 nM [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR185" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR185">185</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par38" class="Para">SMYD2 is a SET domain-containing methyltransferase with a less restricted substrate-specificity. H3K4 and H3K36 as well as non-histone proteins such as HSP90 and p53 were found to be a substrate of SMYD2. Because of the observed overexpression in a variety of cancer types [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR186" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR186">186</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR187" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR187">187</a></span>], it is implicated as a drug target. The first SMYD2 specific inhibitor AZ505 (28, Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig10">10b</a></span>) was identified in a high-throughput screening, showing an enzyme IC<sub>50</sub> of 120 nM as well as selectivity indices of &gt;690-fold [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR188" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR188">188</a></span>]. The steady-state kinetic studies indicated that this compound is competitive to the peptide substrate and uncompetitive for the cofactor SAM, which was confirmed by an X-ray crystallographic investigation of the SMYD2-28 complex. LLY-507 (29) was disclosed to be a more potent inhibitor of SMYD2 (IC<sub>50</sub> &lt; 15 nM), together with &gt;100-fold selectivity over other KMTs [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR189" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR189">189</a></span>]. Compound 29 inhibited cellular methylation with an EC<sub>50</sub> of ~600 nM, showing it is a good chemical probe for investigation of the functions of SMYD2 in normal physiology and diseases.</p>
              </section><section id="Sec19" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">Inhibitors of PRMTs</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par39" class="Para">There are two types of PRMTs [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR9" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR9">9</a></span>]. The majority of PRMTs (including PRMT1-4, 6, and 8) are the type I enzymes, catalyzing mono- and asymmetric di-methylation of an arginine sidechain. PRMT5 is the only known type II enzyme, catalyzing symmetric di-methylation of an arginine.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par40" class="Para">CMPD-1 (30, Fig.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig10">10c</a></span>) is a potent inhibitor of PRMT4 (also known as CARM1) with an enzyme IC<sub>50</sub> of 30 nM as well as selectivity indices of &gt;300-fold against PRMT1 and 3 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR190" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR190">190</a></span>]. X-ray crystallography showed that compound 30 occupies the substrate-binding pocket and the high selectivity arises from sequence differences between PRMT4 and PRMT1/3.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par41" class="Para">An allosteric inhibitor SGC707 (31) is a highly potent inhibitor of PRMT3 an IC<sub>50</sub> of 31 nM [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR191" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR191">191</a></span>]. It exhibited no inhibitory activity against a panel of 27 protein methyltransferases. Crystallographic studies of the PRMT3-31 complex revealed that the inhibitor occupies a cavity &gt;15&nbsp;Å from the enzyme active site. In cellular assay, compound 31 was able to reduce H4R3me2 with an EC<sub>50</sub> of 225 nM.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par42" class="Para">EPZ020411 (32) has been reported to inhibit PRMT6 with an IC<sub>50</sub> of 10 nM, while it exhibited good selectivity (&gt;10 fold) against PRMT1 and 8 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR192" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR192">192</a></span>]. In cellular assays, treatment with 32 resulted in a dose-dependent decrease of H3R2me2 with an EC<sub>50</sub> of 640 nM.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par43" class="Para">Besides the above selective inhibitors against an individual PRMT enzyme, it is of interest that MS023 (33) is a pan-inhibitor of type I PRMTs [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR193" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR193">193</a></span>]. It was designed based on the structures of PRMT4 inhibitor 30 and PRMT6 inhibitor 32. Compound 33 exhibited an enzyme IC<sub>50</sub> of 30, 119, 83, 4, and 5 nM against PRMT1, 3, 4, 6, and 8, respectively. Treatment with 33 significantly decreased levels of H3R2me2 and H4R3me2 with EC<sub>50</sub>s of 56 and 9 nM, respectively.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par44" class="Para">EPZ015666 (34) was reported as a potent and selective inhibitor of PRMT5 (IC<sub>50</sub> = 22 nM), a type II PRMT [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR194" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR194">194</a></span>]. Compound 34 demonstrated potent anti-proliferative activity against mantle cell lymphoma cells Z-138 and Maver-1 (EC<sub>50</sub> = 96 and 450 nM, respectively), because of overexpression of PRMT5 in this type of tumor. It also showed in vivo antitumor activity in xenograft mouse models of these two lymphoma cells.</p>
              </section><section id="Sec20" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">Inhibitors of other KDMs</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par45" class="Para">Despite a number of compounds were reported to be inhibitors of the JmjC family of KDMs, they are either analogs of α-KG or metal chelators (e.g., hydroxamates and pyridinyl carboxylates) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR16" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR16">16</a></span>]. These compounds generally lack potent inhibitory activity (mostly having μM IC<sub>50</sub>s) and enzyme selectivity. In addition, cellular activities, such as histone methylation changes, anti-proliferation and/or toxicity, of these compounds have not been well investigated. Recently, GSK-J1 (35) was reported to be a selective inhibitor of KDM6, responsible for demethylation of H3K27. Compound 35 was initially identified as a lead in a high-throughput, fluorescence based AlphaScreen assay and later confirmed to selectively inhibit KDM6B with an IC<sub>50</sub> of 5&nbsp;μM, while it exhibited ~10-fold selectivity against KDM4 enzymes [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR195" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR195">195</a></span>]. X-ray crystallography showed 35 binds to the active site of the protein with two of its N atoms chelating the metal ion. Compound 35 is not cell permeable, while its ester prodrug GSK-J4 was found to inhibit cellular production of TNF-α with an EC<sub>50</sub> of 9&nbsp;μM. While the global histone methylation changes were not determined, GSK-J4 inhibited a reduction of the H3K27me3 level at the <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">TNFA</em> gene promoter using chromatin immunoprecipitation (ChIP) assays, suggesting it is on-target in cells.</p>
              </section></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec21" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Perspectives and conclusions</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par46" class="Para">Histone methylation plays critical roles in gene regulation, cell differentiation, DNA recombination and damage repair. Histone methylation modifying enzymes, which are often part of a transcription complex, have been heavily studied for the past few decades and great amount of understanding has been obtained for their biochemistry, structures and biological functions in normal physiology as well as in pathogenesis. Many of these enzymes have been validated or implicated as drug targets for intervention. Small molecule inhibitors of these proteins are not only potential therapeutics, but also useful chemical probes for investigation of biological functions of histone modifying enzymes. To date, discovery and development of small molecule inhibitors of HMTs and KDMs have been still in a very early stage, with significant efforts and successful stories from the academia and pharmaceutical industry occurring only in the past few years. However, it is exciting that the field has been growing exponentially: as described in this review, highly potent and selective inhibitors of several such proteins, including DOT1L, LSD1, EZH2, and mutant IDH, have been developed and undergone extensive preclinical testing. Several compounds have quickly entered and been evaluated in clinical trials in just a few years. They have demonstrated promising clinical safety and efficacy, showing great potentials to become clinically useful drugs. These remarkable outcomes could further attract more scientists to the field and jump-start drug discovery and development endeavors targeting histone methylation for cancer therapy. However, due to important functions of histone methylation in normal physiology, inhibition of histone methylation enzymes could cause toxicities. Vigorous toxicological studies should be performed to ensure there is a sufficient therapeutic window for such pharmacological inhibition.</p><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par47" class="Para">Additionally, small molecule compounds targeting histone methylation enzymes could provide great opportunities for finding new cancer biology. Given the differences between pharmacological inhibition and genetic knockdown, using these compounds as probes can complement biological means to explore the functions of a histone methylation protein in health and diseases. For example, many of these proteins (e.g., MLL and DOT1L) contains multiple domains having more functions than being a methyltransferase. Pharmacological inhibition of the catalytic domain of the protein, without disturbing the functions of other domains, could provide more useful insight. Moreover, in the context of cancer therapy, overexpression of a certain histone methylation protein is often found in cancer. However, it is still unclear whether the cancer is dependent upon the catalytic activity of such protein. These compounds can be used to validate whether the protein is a real drug target. Many of the inhibitors shown in Figs.&nbsp;<span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig4">4</a></span>, <span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig5">5</a></span>, <span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig6">6</a></span>, <span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig7">7</a></span>, <span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig8">8</a></span>, <span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig9">9</a></span>, and <span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig10">10</a></span> are commercially available for these studies. The availability and costs can be found in PubChem (<span class="ExternalRef"><a href="https://pubchem.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/"><span class="RefSource">https://pubchem.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/</span></a></span>).</p><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par48" class="Para">There are still a number of challenges in the field from the perspectives of chemistry and biology. From the chemistry side, finding potent and selective small molecule inhibitors is a major challenge, because these enzymes share a relatively high homology and use the same cofactor. This requires an enormous amount of coordinated efforts in high-throughput screening, ligand and structure-based inhibitor design and synthesis, medicinal chemistry as well as biological activity testing. If successful, this is, however, the first step towards yielding a clinically useful drug. Repeated rounds of medicinal chemistry, in vitro and in vivo pharmacokinetics, toxicity and efficacy testing are needed to find suitable drug candidates, which is followed by a low success rate in clinical trials. Very high costs for the whole process represent another barrier. From the biology side, more studies need to be performed to validate whether a histone modifying enzyme is a cancer target. Because of the high costs for high-throughput screening and medicinal chemistry, it is desirable that sufficient evidence showing a critical function of the enzyme has been found in the disease before compound development. Genetic screening of gene mutations in clinical tumor samples represents a powerful means to identify potential candidates (e.g., work in 5.1 and 6.1 for mutations of EZH2 and IDH and [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR196" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR196">196</a></span>]). Ensuing biochemical and biological studies are needed to further identify the links between the functions of such mutation and pathogenesis. In addition, determination and comparative analysis of methylation levels at different histone residues (e.g., H3K79me2 in [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR67" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR67">67</a></span>]) between normal and patient samples could provide useful hints. If there are available resources, direct screening a collection of specific inhibitors of histone modifying enzymes against a large number of cancer cell lines is also a fruitful measure to find such links (e.g., work in [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR103" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR103">103</a></span>]). With the successful stories summarized here, it is envisioned that more histone methylation enzymes can be identified as therapeutic targets for cancer.</p><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par49" class="Para">Combination therapy is a viable approach to increase the effectiveness of a histone methylation modulator. Because multiple histone modifications that change global gene expression might be involved in a certain type of cancer, combination therapy targeting two or more closely associated epigenetic changes could be synergistic. For example, strong synergy was observed for combination treatment using inhibitors of LSD1 and DOT1L that closely associated in MLL-rearranged leukemia [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR111" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR111">111</a></span>]. Synergy was also found for combination therapy of a LSD1 inhibitor with an HDAC inhibitor in vitro and in AML mouse models [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR107" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR107">107</a></span>]. Aberrant methylation of DNA and histone are often associated [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR197" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR197">197</a></span>–<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR199" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR199">199</a></span>], combination with a DNA methylation inhibitor could also be useful. To rationally design a synergistic combination therapy, mechanistic studies of oncogenesis at the molecular level are helpful to find two possible candidate proteins for intervention [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR200" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR200">200</a></span>]. In addition, combination therapy is useful to overcome drug resistance as well as reduce toxicities and side effects.</p></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec22" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Abbreviations</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par50" class="Para">AML, acute myeloid leukemia; ALL, acute lymphocytic leukemia; DOT1L, disruptor of telomeric silencing 1 like; D2HG, D-2-hydroxyglutaric acid; DNMT, DNA methyltransferase; EZH2, Enhancer-of-zeste homolog 2; FAD, flavin adenine dinucleotide; H3K4, histone3-lysine4; H3K79, histone3-lysine79; HDAC, histone deacetylases; HMT, histone methyltransferase; HKMT, histone lysine methyltransferase; IDH, isocitrate dehydrogenases; KDM, lysine demethylase; α-KG, α-ketoglutarate; MAO, monoamine oxidase; MLL, mixed lineage leukemia; LSD1, lysine-specific demethylase 1; PHF, plant homeodomain finger; PRC2, polycomb repressive complex 2; PRMT, protein arginine methyltransferase; SAH, S-adenosylhomocysteine; SAM, S-adenosylmethionine; SET, su(var)3-9, Enhancer-of-zeste, trithorax; WT, wild-type</p></div></section>
